    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1572640008" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1572640008" />
    <a title="Quo" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7392004-quo"><img alt="Quo" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1393910036p2/7392004.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1572640008?book_show_action=true">Mar 07, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Quo" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Quo" href="/user/show/7392004-quo">Quo</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/20934426-snow-country">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Readers interested in Japan/Asia &amp;/or the concept of haiku translated into prose </span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7392004-quo?shelf=interpersonal-dynamics">interpersonal-dynamics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7392004-quo?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7392004-quo?shelf=personal-identity">personal-identity</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1572640008" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11074155222252606529"><i>Snow Country</i> by Yasunari Kawabata is a brief novel where small details ultimately loom large, a story with a very Japanese feel to it, set about 75 years ago. The pace is very slow & at times I felt as if I had entered a kind of snow globe, becoming a spectator to a drama where much more is left unsaid than is actually stated by the main characters. I found it necessary to slow down one's reading pace a bit and to try to savor each moment, each shade of color, every sound, as the action is minim</span>
  <span id="freeText11074155222252606529" style="display:none"><i>Snow Country</i> by Yasunari Kawabata is a brief novel where small details ultimately loom large, a story with a very Japanese feel to it, set about 75 years ago. The pace is very slow &amp; at times I felt as if I had entered a kind of snow globe, becoming a spectator to a drama where much more is left unsaid than is actually stated by the main characters. I found it necessary to slow down one's reading pace a bit and to try to savor each moment, each shade of color, every sound, as the action is minimal but there are strongly implied interactions between the two main characters, Shimamura &amp; Komako, largely defined by their respective class boundaries. <br><br>Shimamura is from a wealthy family, so much so that he has no need of a job and considerable time to take refuge at a snow country lodge, while Komako is largely self-taught, acting in a freelance manner &amp; attempting to take on the role of a hot springs or "mountain geisha" without the formal training that would be a necessary prelude &amp; which would convey a much higher status as geisha in Kyoto or Tokyo during the period when the story takes place. The two meet at a hot springs, snow country inn and in the course of several visits by Shimamura, form an intimate bond but one where both fully understand the limitations of their relationship. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1578438309i/28733284.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Shimamura is married &amp; a father but not very much defined by the relationship with his wife, who is apparently accustomed to her husband's absences. He is called a dilettante in the translator's introduction to <i>Snow Country</i> but I prefer to think of him as a would-be connoisseur, a dabbler or an aesthete, someone in search of identity of the sort that one might normally get in part through one's professional training. <br><br>Shimamura translates French treatises on the dance for the golden age of Russian ballet, not something that will gain him any recognition or contribute in any way to the world of Japanese dance. Rather, if pressed, he might say that his interest would bring "aid &amp; comfort only to Shimamura." Beyond that, the author tells us that "Shimamura pampered himself with the somewhat whimsical pleasure of sneering at himself through his work and it may have been from such a pleasure that his sad little dream world sprang." <br><br>In time, Shimamura's connection to Komako becomes almost addictive, with one day drifting into the next even though he wonders what is lacking in his behavior that makes it difficult if not impossible to break free. "He stood gazing at his own coldness, so to speak." At the same time, Shimamura it seems is at least minimally sensitive to Komako's needs, feeling that she had lost herself in the relationship. <blockquote>All of Komako came to him but it seemed that nothing went out from him to her. He heard in his chest, like snow piling up, the sound of Komako, an echo beating against empty walls. And he knew that he could not go on pampering himself forever.</blockquote>There is another strong fixation for Shimamura and that is the character of Yoko, a young woman who appears almost lifted from a dream &amp; whose presence forms a counterpoint to that of Komako, almost standing for an element that from a distance seems essential but is beyond the reach of Shimamura and the readers of <i>Snow Country</i> as well. <br><br>In fact, in a way, much of the book seems slightly veiled and one certainly has to develop one's own scenario for the ending but somehow this lack of finality seems appropriate. Beyond that, Kawabata's use of light &amp; color is often stunning, with the varying shades of gray sometimes <br>almost dynamic. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1578438309i/28733282.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>In reading a few reviews of <i>Snow Country</i> at this site, there seems little middle ground as to the appraisals. Some readers appear to clamor for a definitive ending and a more familiar (western) orientation. Personally, I think it is of some benefit to have gleaned details about the author's life and prevalent Japanese traditions as well. for example, Yasunari Kawabata grew up without the warmth of his parents who both died when he was quite young. He was sent to live with grandparents, who also died as did his sister, raised separately &amp; who he saw only once following the death of his parents. Since the author speaks of an inability to form relationships in this novel, his own lack of strong family bonds would seem to be very telling. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1578438309i/28733283.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Lastly, the snow country hotel where the novel is set still exists and there is a kind of shrine/museum to Kawabata at the Echigo-yuzawa, Onsen Yuzawa Toei Hotel. *If interested in making a pilgrimage there, rooms are $96 per person, per day (at the time of this review), with a full meal plan (buffet breakfast + dinner) included in the tariff. However, the only remaining snow country geisha is in her 80s at this point and she charges a fixed price for an interview, with a translator an extra expense.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11074155222252606529" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2300517752" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2300517752" />
    <a title="Bob Newman" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/72341080-bob-newman"><img alt="Bob Newman" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2300517752?book_show_action=true">Feb 18, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bob Newman" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bob Newman" href="/user/show/72341080-bob-newman">Bob Newman</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/72341080-bob-newman?shelf=japanese-literature">japanese-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2300517752" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5592229970444687799">A Rake's Non-Progress<br/><br/>I studied Japanese for four years in college and as a senior, more years ago than I care to count, I read this novel in Japanese, one of only two I ever made it through. Recently, having forgotten everything about the novel and because I have forgotten too many characters to read in Japanese anymore, I re-read it in English. SNOW COUNTRY is nothing if not a strange work. At the risk of sounding snobbish or whatever, I have to say that it is stranger in English than in Japane</span>
  <span id="freeText5592229970444687799" style="display:none">A Rake's Non-Progress<br /><br />I studied Japanese for four years in college and as a senior, more years ago than I care to count, I read this novel in Japanese, one of only two I ever made it through. Recently, having forgotten everything about the novel and because I have forgotten too many characters to read in Japanese anymore, I re-read it in English. SNOW COUNTRY is nothing if not a strange work. At the risk of sounding snobbish or whatever, I have to say that it is stranger in English than in Japanese. Japanese allows for a great deal of vagueness and reading between the lines. English prefers definite words about definite events. So, as I read the novel again, I did wonder why I had found it so enthralling the first time and concluded that language had something to do with it. Nothing is definite. As usual, Kawabata is not strong on plot. An idle playboy-type (we never learn where his money comes from) visits a resort in the mountains of Western Japan, facing the Japan Sea, the snowiest region of the world. He meets an offbeat sort of geisha, Komako. He rather likes her: she likes him, maybe more than that, but the relationship is fragile. Everything is extremely vague, the surroundings are beautiful, and as always, the reader can enjoy the Japanese fascination with the tiniest details of the natural world. Komako, available and prone to drink, is contrasted to the distant Yoko, a pure girl, with a beautiful voice, a girl the protagonist views only from afar. She shows devotion to one man and to duty. The end perhaps underlines Kawabata's view of postwar society, his disappointment at what Japan had become. If you have read "Memoirs of a Geisha", this might be a satisfactory antidote---not that the former was bad, but it is an American viewpoint. This completely Japanese view of a geisha could be more realistic in terms of what the average geisha's life would have been like in the provinces, far from the splendid inns of Kyoto and Tokyo. If you like haiku, Mondrian, minimalist photography, you would like this novel. If however, your taste is Faulkner, Zola, Balzac, the Russians, then I doubt if you would enjoy SNOW COUNTRY.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5592229970444687799" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1415315265" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1415315265" />
    <a title="Nandakishore Varma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6237864-nandakishore-varma"><img alt="Nandakishore Varma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1583648733p2/6237864.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1415315265?book_show_action=true">Oct 12, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nandakishore Varma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nandakishore Varma" href="/user/show/6237864-nandakishore-varma">Nandakishore Varma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1415315265" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17772702536108329906">Most of my friends from Kerala would be familiar with the film <a href='https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thoovanathumbikal' rel='nofollow noopener'>Thoovanathumbikal</a> by the famous Malayalam writer and director P. Padmarajan. The film narrates the story of the love of a young-man-about-town, Jayakrishnan, for two girls: Radha, a prim-and-proper Indian miss and Clara, a prostitute. Padmarajan uses the two women as symbols for two facets of femininity (and therefore, of life) - one light and chaste and the other dark and mysterious. I was reminded of this movie all the time while r</span>
  <span id="freeText17772702536108329906" style="display:none">Most of my friends from Kerala would be familiar with the film <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thoovanathumbikal" rel="nofollow noopener">Thoovanathumbikal</a> by the famous Malayalam writer and director P. Padmarajan. The film narrates the story of the love of a young-man-about-town, Jayakrishnan, for two girls: Radha, a prim-and-proper Indian miss and Clara, a prostitute. Padmarajan uses the two women as symbols for two facets of femininity (and therefore, of life) - one light and chaste and the other dark and mysterious. I was reminded of this movie all the time while reading <i>The Snow Country</i>. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1444896120i/16570982._SX540_.jpg" width="360" height="240" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Of course, apart from the basic similarity in theme, the stories have no relation. Kawabata's protagonist, the idler Shimamura, is a dilettante who dabbles in occidental dance without actually having watched a single performance. He approaches women with the same superficial attitude. In Japan's snow country, the part of the island which falls west of the central mountain range and is caressed by the winds from Siberia, Shimamura has an affair with a geisha named Komako. For him, it's just a way to pass time - but she is in right earnest.One can see it is doomed from the start.<br><br>Shimamura's attraction towards Komako is in some way intrinsically tied up with his obsession for Yoko, the nurse attending the terminally ill son of the music teacher Komako is living with. However, it is hinted that she is much more than a nurse: in fact, her attachment to this sick boy leads to the formation of a love-hate bond between Yoko and Komako, as the latter was also enamoured of him once and embraced the profession of geisha to support him. In another fashion, Shimamura is also tied to the two women - one physically, and the other in the realm of imagination.<br><br>What raises this novel above the humdrum level of the run-of-the-mill romance is Kawabata's use of metaphor and the way the novel is structured. There is very little philosophising. The language is very pictorial, and in some places, one can easily make a movie in one's own mind. I was especially entranced by the description of the reflection of Yoko's eyes in the train window; and the juxtaposition of the same scene with Komako's reflection in the misted mirror in Shimamura's hotel room. Also, the descriptions reminded me of Japanese prints of the Japan Sea and Fujiyama with their spare lines and evocative visual impact.<br><br>The "snow country" here is very real; and Komako, the "hot spring geisha" (considered little more than a prostitute by other geisha, if one is to believe Wikipedia), an expression of it. She is alternatively hot and cold, and red and white - drunk and wanton in the night, cold and aloof during daytime - the symbol of a landscape enticing and forbidden at the same time. This is the threshold of the Goddess where man must stand as an outsider forever. She is as unattainable as Yoko, yet somehow interlinked <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[(a link finally sealed symbolically with fire, as Komakao carries Yoko from the burning movie house) <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1444896120i/16570983._SX540_.jpg" width="360" height="240" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>But the story somehow did not speak to me. I think it's entirely personal, an no reflection on the quality of the novel - however, as rankings are personal, I can give this only three stars.</["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="17772702536108329906" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1174268178" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1174268178" />
    <a title="Mizuki" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16842102-mizuki"><img alt="Mizuki" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1368277474p2/16842102.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1174268178?book_show_action=true">Feb 06, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mizuki" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mizuki" href="/user/show/16842102-mizuki">Mizuki</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789573902003" href="/book/show/27191470">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16842102-mizuki?shelf=chinese-translation">chinese-translation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16842102-mizuki?shelf=japanese-authors">japanese-authors</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16842102-mizuki?shelf=it-is-not-the-book-it-is-me">it-is-not-the-book-it-is-me</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16842102-mizuki?shelf=pretty-good">pretty-good</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1174268178" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13464797549032419958">At first I found it difficult to know where to put this book and what to expect from it. <br/><br/>We have three main characters: a well off, cultured, married middle age man who travels from Tokyo to the 'Snow Country' (a remote hot springs village in the far North and its surrounding); the man then meets a young woman (who later becomes a geisha due to livelihood problems) and the two of them develop a relationship almost instantly. As time pasts and seasons change, the middle age man travels to the 'Sn</span>
  <span id="freeText13464797549032419958" style="display:none">At first I found it difficult to know where to put this book and what to expect from it. <br /><br />We have three main characters: a well off, cultured, married middle age man who travels from Tokyo to the 'Snow Country' (a remote hot springs village in the far North and its surrounding); the man then meets a young woman (who later becomes a geisha due to livelihood problems) and the two of them develop a relationship almost instantly. As time pasts and seasons change, the middle age man travels to the 'Snow Country' to visit his lover/mistress now and then, their relationship.......to be frank is going nowhere. Later the man becomes attracted to another young woman in the village and in the end, a freak accident takes place, breaking off this love triangle. <br /><br />The plot sounds nothing special (love triangle again!) and at the beginning of the story I struggled to understand what is going on between this man and the geisha: <i>"what does she see in this man who asked her to find him some hooker to sleep with?"</i> <br /><br />But by the ending part of the story, my sympathy to the geisha grows, through the skillfully written interaction between this couple, the geisha's hopeless love toward the man, the argument they have, her struggle for survival, her loneliness (her mentor and family are all dead), her sadness and jealousy toward this other younger woman all start to make sense to me. And the sadder thing probably is, in the end every character's love and obsession has come to nothing. <br /><br />As mentioned above, there is a younger woman who drifts into the love relationship between the couple, but to me this younger woman (with clear eyes and voice so beautiful that it is close to sadness itself) is more like a faerie-like representative of fragile beauty than an actual character.<br /><br />'Nothingness' and 'in vain' most likely is the lurking main theme of <i>Snow Country</i>: it is a story about the quiet, breathtaking beauty of the Japanese landscapes and the four seasons; a story about how love doesn't save anyone from their fates and from themselves in the end.<br /><br />I guess it isn't uncommon for us to be visited by this feeling/insight: life is fragile, beauty never last long, love is fleeing and ever-changing. Perhaps that's what the author tried to say about life, love, nature and beauty?<br /><br />PS: I plan to visit Japan later this year, and that's the main reason why I picked up this book, despite the few problems I have with the story, this book still motivates me into reading more of Yasunari Kawabata's novels. So, 3.75 stars.<br /><br />PSS: <i>And Then</i> by Natsume Sōseki (<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/169438.And_Then">https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1...</a>) is another masterfully written story about flawed characters and their doomed love, and how love actually doesn't save anyone from themselves.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13464797549032419958" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('qzbNuHPXfviltVvJb/Zf8FgAY+szt4ng4xOuREVubm+REKRqyIm3kAKe76iYA1Uvjl+EI/06B4e4bej1iC2c9Q==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('lBjWqws/sNK1edVirlR3Ww3pu5ikDd9ItgeaQhhUNEOuPr95sGF5uhJSYQNZoX2E27ZcUGqAUS/tedzz1RfG2Q==')})
  }

      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_62798'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/62798.The_Temple_of_the_Golden_Pavilion?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">The Temple of the Golden Pavilion<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/35258.Yukio_Mishima?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Yukio Mishima<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.98 avg rating &mdash; 12,169 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1956\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer13298140979789367176\"> In The Temple of the Golden Pavilion, celebrated Japanese novelist Yukio Mishima creates a haunting and vivid portrait of a young man’s obsession with idealized beauty and his destructive quest to possess it fully.\n\nMizoguchi, an ostracized stuttere<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText13298140979789367176\" style=\"display:none\"> In The Temple of the Golden Pavilion, celebrated Japanese novelist Yukio Mishima creates a haunting and vivid portrait of a young man’s obsession with idealized beauty and his destructive quest to possess it fully.\n\nMizoguchi, an ostracized stutterer, develops a childhood fascination with Kyoto’s famous Golden Temple. While an acolyte at the temple, he fixates on the structure’s aesthetic perfection and it becomes the one and only object of his desire. But as Mizoguchi begins to perceive flaws in the temple, he determines that the only true path to beauty lies in an act of horrendous violence. Based on a real incident that occurred in 1950, The Temple of the Golden Pavilion brilliantly portrays the passions and agonies of a young man in postwar Japan, bringing to the subject the erotic imagination and instinct for the dramatic moment that marked Mishima as one of the towering makers of modern fiction. With an introduction by Donald Keene; Translated from the Japanese by Ivan Morris.(Book Jacket Status: Jacketed)<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"13298140979789367176\" href=\"#\" Osamu Dazai’s The Setting Sun takes this milieu as its background to tell the story of the decline of a minor aristocratic family.\n\nThe story is told through the eyes of Kazuko, the unmarried daughter of a widowed aristocrat. Her search for self meaning in a society devoid of use for her forms the crux of Dazai’s novel. It is a sad story, and structurally is a novel very much within the confines of the Japanese take on the novel in a way reminiscent of authors such as Nobel Prize winner Yasunari Kawabata – the social interactions are peripheral and understated, nuances must be drawn, and for readers more used to Western novelistic forms this comes across as being rather wishy-washy.\n\nKazuko’s mother falls ill, and due to their financial circumstances they are forced to take a cottage in the countryside. Her brother, who became addicted to opium during the war is missing. When he returns, Kazuko attempts to form a liaison with the novelist Uehara. The web that his insatiable lust spun about him soon trapped another young woman, and another ... and the relationships between the women thus caught were to form, over the years, a subtle, shifting pattern in which they all played a part. There was Suga, the innocent, introspective girl from a respectable but impoverished family; the outgoing, cheerful, almost boyish Yumi; the flirtatious, seductive Miya, who soon found her father-in-law more dependable as a man than his brutish son.... And at the center, rejected yet dominating them all, the near tragic figure of the wife Tomo, whose passionate heart was always, until that final day, held in check by an old-fashioned code.\n\nIn a series of colorful, unforgettable scenes, Enchi brilliantly handles the human interplay within the ill-fated Shirakawa family. Japan\'s leading woman novelist and a member of the prestigious Art Academy, she combines a graceful, evocative style that consciously echoes the Tale of Genji with keen insight and an impressive ability to develop her characters over a long period of time. Her work is rooted deep in the female psychology, and it is her women above all-so clearly differentiated yet all so utterly feminine-who live in the memory. With The Waiting Years, a new and important literary figure makes her debut in the Western world.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"10731880048494352936\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer3959817363771714949">Yasunari <b>Kawabata</b> (<a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/846386._' rel='nofollow noopener'>川端 康成</a>) was a Japanese short story writer and novelist whose spare, lyrical, subtly-shaded prose works won him the Nobel Prize for Literature in 1968, the first Japanese author to receive the award. His works have enjoyed broad international appeal and are still widely read today.<br/><br/>Nobel Lecture: 1968<br/><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://www.nobelprize.org/nobel_prizes/literature/laureates/1968/kawabata-lecture.html'>http://www.nobelprize.org/nobel_prize...</a></span>
  <span id="freeText3959817363771714949" style="display:none">Yasunari <b>Kawabata</b> (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/846386._" rel="nofollow noopener">川端 康成</a>) was a Japanese short story writer and novelist whose spare, lyrical, subtly-shaded prose works won him the Nobel Prize for Literature in 1968, the first Japanese author to receive the award. His works have enjoyed broad international appeal and are still widely read today.<br /><br />Nobel Lecture: 1968<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.nobelprize.org/nobel_prizes/literature/laureates/1968/kawabata-lecture.html">http://www.nobelprize.org/nobel_prize...</a></span>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/1855151-yukiguni">Quotes from Snow Country</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;As he caught his footing, his head fell back, and the Milky Way flowed down inside him with a roar.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/865773-as-he-caught-his-footing-his-head-fell-back-and">1088 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;But even more than her diary, Shimamura was surprised at her statement that she had carefully cataloged every novel and short story she had read since she was fifteen or sixteen. The record already filled ten notebooks.<br />"You write down your criticisms, do you?"<br />"I could never do anything like that. I just write down the author and the characters and how they are related to each other. That is about all."<br />"But what good does it do?"<br />"None at all."<br />"A waste of effort."<br />"A complete waste of effort," she answered brightly, as though the admission meant little to her. She gazed solemnly at Shimamura, however.<br />A complete waste of effort. For some reason Shimamura wanted to stress the point. But, drawn to her at that moment, he felt a quiet like the voice of the rain flow over him. He knew well enough that for her it was in fact no waste of effort, but somehow the final determination that it had the effect of distilling and purifying the woman's existence.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/108184-but-even-more-than-her-diary-shimamura-was-surprised-at">51 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
